<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Central text of Rabbinic Judaism</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Babylonian Talmud. For the Jerusalem Talmud, see <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Talmud" title="Jerusalem Talmud">Jerusalem Talmud</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Talmudic" redirects here. "Talmudic Aramaic" refers to the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Babylonian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic">Jewish Babylonian Aramaic</a> as found in the Talmud.</div>

<p>The <b>Talmud</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/Ê/: 'u' in 'push'">Ê</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/Ã¦/: 'a' in 'bad'">Ã¦</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span></span>-/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020748213">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-family:"SBL Hebrew","SBL BibLit","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey David CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">×ªÖ·Ö¼×Ö°××Ö¼×</span>â</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Hebrew" title="Romanization of Hebrew">romanized</a>:Â </small><span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">TÃ¡lmÅ«á¸</i></span>) is the central text of <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a> and the primary source of Jewish religious law (<i><a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakha</a></i>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_theology" title="Jewish theology">Jewish theology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steinsaltz_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steinsaltz-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neusner_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neusner-2">[2]</a></sup> Until the advent of <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>, in nearly all Jewish communities, the Talmud was the centerpiece of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_culture" title="Jewish culture">Jewish cultural life</a> and was foundational to "all Jewish thought and aspirations", serving also as "the guide for the daily life" of Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safrai_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Safrai-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>Talmud</i> normally refers to the collection of writings named specifically the <b>Babylonian Talmud</b> (<span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">Talmud Bavli</i></span>), although there is also an earlier collection known as the <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Talmud" title="Jerusalem Talmud">Jerusalem Talmud</a> (<span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">Talmud Yerushalmi</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It may also traditionally be called <b><span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">Shas</i></span></b> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="he">×©×´×¡</span></span>), a <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> abbreviation of <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">shisha sedarim</i></span>, or the "six orders" of the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a>.
</p><p>The Talmud has two components: the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="he">××©× ×</span></span>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 200 CE), a written <a href="/wiki/Compendium" title="Compendium">compendium</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">Oral Torah</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Gemara" title="Gemara">Gemara</a> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="he">×××¨×</span></span>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 500 CE), an <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/elucidation" title="wikt:elucidation">elucidation</a> of the Mishnah and related <a href="/wiki/Tannaim" title="Tannaim">Tannaitic</a> writings that often ventures onto other subjects and expounds broadly on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Hebrew Bible</a>. The term "Talmud" may refer to either the Gemara alone, or the Mishnah and Gemara together.
</p><p>The entire Talmud consists of 63 <a href="/wiki/Tractate" title="Tractate">tractates</a>, and in the standard print, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vilna_Shas" title="Vilna Shas">Vilna Shas</a>, there are 2,711 double-sided folios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> It is written in <a href="/wiki/Mishnaic_Hebrew" title="Mishnaic Hebrew">Mishnaic Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Babylonian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic">Jewish Babylonian Aramaic</a> and contains the teachings and opinions of thousands of <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbis</a> (dating from before the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a> through to the fifth century) on a variety of subjects, including <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_ethics" title="Jewish ethics">Jewish ethics</a>, philosophy, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_customs" title="Jewish customs">customs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_history" title="Jewish history">history</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_folklore" title="Jewish folklore">folklore</a>, and many other topics. The Talmud is the basis for all codes of Jewish law and is widely quoted in <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">rabbinic literature</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Talmud translates as "instruction, learning", from the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">Semitic root</a> <i><span title="Hebrew-language text"><i lang="he-Latn"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D7%9C%D7%9E%D7%93" title="wikt:×××">LMD</a></i></span></i>, meaning "teach, study".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">Oral Torah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Labeled_talmud.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1206" data-file-width="828" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Labeled_talmud.png/220px-Labeled_talmud.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Labeled_talmud.png/330px-Labeled_talmud.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Labeled_talmud.png/440px-Labeled_talmud.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Labeled_talmud.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oz_Vehadar" title="Oz Vehadar">Oz veHadar</a> edition of the first page of the Babylonian Talmud, with elements numbered in a spiraling rainbowË (1) <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Boaz_ben_Simon_Baruch" title="Joshua Boaz ben Simon Baruch">Joshua Boaz ben Simon Baruch</a>'s <i>Mesorat haShas</i>, (2) <a href="/wiki/Joel_Sirkis" title="Joel Sirkis">Joel Sirkis</a>'s <i>Hagahot</i> (3) <a href="/wiki/Akiva_Eiger" title="Akiva Eiger">Akiva Eiger</a>'s <i>Gilyon haShas</i>, (4) Completion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solomon_ben_Isaac" title="Solomon ben Isaac">Solomon ben Isaac</a>'s commentary from the Soncino printing, (5) <a href="/wiki/Nissim_ben_Jacob" title="Nissim ben Jacob">Nissim ben Jacob</a>'s commentary, (6) <a href="/wiki/Chananel_ben_Chushiel" title="Chananel ben Chushiel">Hananel ben Hushiel</a>'s commentary, (7) a survey of the verses quoted, (8) <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Boaz_ben_Simon_Baruch" title="Joshua Boaz ben Simon Baruch">Joshua Boaz ben Simon Baruch</a>'s <i>Ein Mishpat/Ner Mitzvah</i>, (9) the folio and page numbers, (10) the <a href="/wiki/Berakhot_(tractate)" title="Berakhot (tractate)">tractate</a> title, (11) the chapter number, (12), the chapter heading, (13), <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Solomon ben Isaac</a>'s commentary, (14) the <a href="/wiki/Tosafot" title="Tosafot">Tosafot</a>, (15) the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a>, (16) the Gemara, (17) an editorial footnote.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1648" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg/220px-Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg/330px-Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg/440px-Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rashi%27s_Talmud_Commentary.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early printing of the Talmud (<a href="/wiki/Ta%27anit" title="Ta'anit">Ta'anit</a> 9b); with commentary by <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a></div></div></div>
<p>Originally, Jewish scholarship was <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral</a> and transferred from one generation to the next. Rabbis expounded and debated the Torah (the written Torah expressed in the Hebrew Bible) and discussed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> without the benefit of written works (other than the Biblical books themselves), though some may have made private notes (<i><span title="Hebrew-language text"><i lang="he-Latn">megillot setarim</i></span></i>), for example, of court decisions.  This situation changed drastically due to the Roman destruction of the Jewish commonwealth and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Second Temple</a> in the year 70 and the consequent upheaval of Jewish social and legal norms. As the rabbis were required to face a new realityâmainly Judaism without a Temple (to serve as the center of teaching and study) and total Roman control over <a href="/wiki/Judaea_(Roman_province)" title="Judaea (Roman province)">Judaea</a>, without at least partial autonomyâthere was a flurry of legal discourse and the old system of oral scholarship could not be maintained. It is during this period that rabbinic discourse began to be recorded in writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest full manuscript of the Talmud, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Munich_Talmud" title="Munich Talmud">Munich Talmud</a> (Codex Hebraicus 95), dates from 1342 and is available online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[c]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Babylonian_and_Jerusalem">Babylonian and Jerusalem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Babylonian and Jerusalem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The process of "Gemara" proceeded in what were then the two major centers of Jewish scholarship, <a href="/wiki/Talmudic_Academies_in_Syria_Palaestina" title="Talmudic Academies in Syria Palaestina">Galilee</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talmudic_Academies_in_Babylonia" title="Talmudic Academies in Babylonia">Babylonia</a>. Correspondingly, two bodies of analysis developed, and two works of Talmud were created. The older compilation is called the Jerusalem Talmud or the <span title="Hebrew-language text"><i lang="he">Talmud Yerushalmi</i></span>. It was compiled in the 4th century in Galilee. The Babylonian Talmud was compiled about the year 500, although it continued to be edited later. The word "Talmud", when used without qualification, usually refers to the Babylonian Talmud.
</p><p>While the editors of Jerusalem Talmud and Babylonian Talmud each mention the other community, most scholars believe these documents were written independently; <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacobs" title="Louis Jacobs">Louis Jacobs</a> writes, "If the editors of either had had access to an actual text of the other, it is inconceivable that they would not have mentioned this. Here the <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_silence" title="Argument from silence">argument from silence</a> is very convincing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jerusalem_Talmud">Jerusalem Talmud</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Jerusalem Talmud">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Talmud" title="Jerusalem Talmud">Jerusalem Talmud</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="523" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg/220px-Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg/330px-Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg/440px-Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yerushalmi_Talmud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page of a medieval Jerusalem Talmud manuscript, from the <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Geniza" title="Cairo Geniza">Cairo Geniza</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Jerusalem Talmud, also known as the Palestinian Talmud, or <span title="Hebrew-language text"><i lang="he">Talmuda de-Eretz Yisrael</i></span> (Talmud of the Land of Israel), was one of the two compilations of Jewish religious teachings and commentary that was transmitted orally for centuries prior to its compilation by Jewish scholars in the <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Israel" title="Land of Israel">Land of Israel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-11">[8]</a></sup> It is a compilation of teachings of the schools of <a href="/wiki/Tiberias" title="Tiberias">Tiberias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sepphoris" title="Sepphoris">Sepphoris</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caesarea" title="Caesarea">Caesarea</a>. It is written largely in <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Palestinian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Palestinian Aramaic">Jewish Palestinian Aramaic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Western_Aramaic_languages" title="Western Aramaic languages">Western Aramaic language</a> that differs from <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Babylonian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic">its Babylonian counterpart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JSTOR_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-JSTOR-12">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Project_Gutenberg_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Project_Gutenberg-13">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>This Talmud is a synopsis of the analysis of the Mishnah that was developed over the course of nearly 200 years by the <a href="/wiki/Talmudic_Academies_in_Syria_Palaestina" title="Talmudic Academies in Syria Palaestina">Academies in Galilee</a> (principally those of Tiberias and Caesarea.) Because of their location, the sages of these Academies devoted considerable attention to the analysis of the agricultural laws of the Land of Israel. Traditionally, this Talmud was thought to have been redacted in about the year 350 by Rav Muna and Rav Yossi in the Land of Israel. It is traditionally known as the <i>Talmud Yerushalmi</i> ("Jerusalem Talmud"), but the name is a misnomer, as it was not prepared in Jerusalem. It has more accurately been called "The Talmud of the Land of Israel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023981488">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .rquote{width:auto!important;float:none!important}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>The eye and the heart are two abettors to the crime.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><a href="/wiki/Chazal" title="Chazal">Yitzhak ben Eleazar</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Talmud" title="Jerusalem Talmud">Jerusalem Talmud</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berakhot_(Talmud)" title="Berakhot (Talmud)">Berakhot</a> 1:5)</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Its final redaction probably belongs to the end of the 4th century, but the individual scholars who brought it to its present form cannot be fixed with assurance. By this time <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> had become the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and Jerusalem the holy city of Christendom. In 325 <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a>, the first Christian emperor, said "let us then have nothing in common with the detestable Jewish crowd."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eusebius_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eusebius-15">[12]</a></sup> This policy made a Jew an outcast and pauper. The compilers of the Jerusalem Talmud consequently lacked the time to produce a work of the quality they had intended. The text is evidently incomplete and is not easy to follow.
</p><p>The apparent cessation of work on the Jerusalem Talmud in the 5th century has been associated with the decision of <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> in 425 to suppress the <a href="/wiki/Nasi_(Hebrew_title)" title="Nasi (Hebrew title)">Patriarchate</a> and put an end to the practice of <a href="/wiki/Semikhah" title="Semikhah">semikhah</a>, formal scholarly ordination. Some modern scholars have questioned this connection.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023981488" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>Just as wisdom has made a crown for one's head, so, too, humility has made a sole for one's foot.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><a href="/wiki/Chazal" title="Chazal">Yitzhak ben Eleazar</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Talmud" title="Jerusalem Talmud">Jerusalem Talmud</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Shabbat_(Talmud)" title="Shabbat (Talmud)">Shabbat</a> 8b)</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Despite its incomplete state, the Jerusalem Talmud remains an indispensable source of knowledge of the development of the Jewish Law in the Holy Land. It was also an important primary source for the study of the Babylonian Talmud by the <a href="/wiki/Kairouan" title="Kairouan">Kairouan</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Chananel_ben_Chushiel" title="Chananel ben Chushiel">Chananel ben Chushiel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nissim_ben_Jacob" title="Nissim ben Jacob">Nissim ben Jacob</a>, with the result that opinions ultimately based on the Jerusalem Talmud found their way into both the <a href="/wiki/Tosafot" title="Tosafot">Tosafot</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">Mishneh Torah</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>. Ethical maxims contained in the Jerusalem Talmud are scattered and interspersed in the legal discussions throughout the several treatises, many of which differing from those in the Babylonian Talmud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the formation of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Israel" title="State of Israel">state of Israel</a> there is some interest in restoring <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> traditions. For example, rabbi <a href="/wiki/David_Bar-Hayim" title="David Bar-Hayim">David Bar-Hayim</a> of the <i>Makhon Shilo</i> institute has issued a <a href="/wiki/Siddur" title="Siddur">siddur</a> reflecting <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> practice as found in the Jerusalem Talmud and other sources.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Babylonian_Talmud">Babylonian Talmud</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Babylonian Talmud">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Talmud_set.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1568" data-file-width="2239" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Talmud_set.JPG/220px-Talmud_set.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Talmud_set.JPG/330px-Talmud_set.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Talmud_set.JPG/440px-Talmud_set.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Talmud_set.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A full set of the Babylonian Talmud</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Babylonian Talmud</b> (<i>Talmud Bavli</i>) consists of documents compiled over the period of <a href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a> (3rd to 6th centuries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> During this time, the most important of the Jewish centres in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, a region called "<a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>" in Jewish sources and later known as <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Nehardea" title="Nehardea">Nehardea</a>, Nisibis (modern <a href="/wiki/Nusaybin" title="Nusaybin">Nusaybin</a>), Mahoza (<a href="/wiki/Al-Mada%27in" title="Al-Mada'in">al-Mada'in</a>, just to the south of what is now <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pumbedita" title="Pumbedita">Pumbedita</a> (near present-day <a href="/wiki/Al_Anbar_Governorate" title="Al Anbar Governorate">al Anbar Governorate</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Sura_Academy" title="Sura Academy">Sura Academy</a>, probably located about 60Â km (37Â mi) south of Baghdad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Babylonian Talmud comprises the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> and the Babylonian Gemara, the latter representing the culmination of more than 300 years of analysis of the Mishnah in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talmudic_Academies_in_Babylonia" title="Talmudic Academies in Babylonia">Talmudic Academies in Babylonia</a>. The foundations of this process of analysis were laid by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abba_Arika" title="Abba Arika">Abba Arika</a> (175â247), a disciple of <a href="/wiki/Judah_ha-Nasi" title="Judah ha-Nasi">Judah ha-Nasi</a>. Tradition ascribes the compilation of the Babylonian Talmud in its present form to two Babylonian sages, <a href="/wiki/Rav_Ashi" title="Rav Ashi">Rav Ashi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ravina_II" title="Ravina II">Ravina II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup> Rav Ashi was president of the Sura Academy from 375 to 427. The work begun by Rav Ashi was completed by Ravina, who is traditionally regarded as the final Amoraic expounder. Accordingly, traditionalists argue that Ravina's death in 475<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> is the latest possible date for the completion of the redaction of the Talmud.  However, even on the most traditional view, a few passages are regarded as the work of a group of rabbis who edited the Talmud after the end of the Amoraic period, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Savoraim" title="Savoraim">Savoraim</a></i> or <i>Rabbanan Savora'e</i> (meaning "reasoners" or "considerers").
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_of_style_and_subject_matter">Comparison of style and subject matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Comparison of style and subject matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are significant differences between the two Talmud compilations.  The language of the Jerusalem Talmud is a western Aramaic dialect, which differs from the form of Aramaic in the Babylonian Talmud. The Talmud Yerushalmi is often fragmentary and difficult to read, even for experienced Talmudists. The redaction of the Talmud Bavli, on the other hand, is more careful and precise. The law as laid down in the two compilations is basically similar, except in emphasis and in minor details. The Jerusalem Talmud has not received much attention from commentators, and such traditional commentaries as exist are mostly concerned with comparing its teachings to those of the Talmud Bavli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JVLTalJ_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-JVLTalJ-21">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neither the Jerusalem nor the Babylonian Talmud covers the entire Mishnah: for example, a Babylonian Gemara exists only for 37 out of the 63 tractates of the Mishnah. In particular:
</p>
<ul><li>The Jerusalem Talmud covers all the tractates of <a href="/wiki/Zeraim" title="Zeraim">Zeraim</a>, while the Babylonian Talmud covers only tractate <a href="/wiki/Berakhot_(tractate)" title="Berakhot (tractate)">Berachot</a>. The reason might be that most laws from the Order Zeraim (agricultural laws limited to the Land of Israel) had little practical relevance in Babylonia and were therefore not included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> The Jerusalem Talmud has a greater focus on the Land of Israel and the Torah's agricultural laws pertaining to the land because it was written in the Land of Israel where the laws applied.</li>
<li>The Jerusalem Talmud does not cover the Mishnaic order of <a href="/wiki/Kodashim" title="Kodashim">Kodashim</a>, which deals with sacrificial rites and laws pertaining to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Temple</a>, while the Babylonian Talmud does cover it.  It is not clear why this is, as the laws were not directly applicable in either country following the Temple's destruction in year 70. Early Rabbinic literature indicates that there once was a Jerusalem Talmud commentary on Kodashim but it has been lost to history (though in the early Twentieth Century an infamous forgery of the lost tractates was at first widely accepted before being quickly exposed).</li>
<li>In both Talmuds, only one tractate of <a href="/wiki/Tohorot" title="Tohorot">Tohorot</a> (ritual purity laws) is examined, that of the menstrual laws, <a href="/wiki/Niddah" title="Niddah">Niddah</a>.</li></ul>
<p>The Babylonian Talmud records the opinions of the rabbis of the <i>Ma'arava</i> (the West, meaning Israel/Palestine) as well as of those of Babylonia, while the Jerusalem Talmud seldom cites the Babylonian rabbis. The Babylonian version also contains the opinions of more generations because of its later date of completion. For both these reasons, it is regarded as a more comprehensive<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> collection of the opinions available. On the other hand, because of the centuries of redaction between the composition of the Jerusalem and the Babylonian Talmud, the opinions of early <i><a href="/wiki/Amoraim" title="Amoraim">amoraim</a></i> might be closer to their original form in the Jerusalem Talmud.
</p><p>The influence of the Babylonian Talmud has been far greater than that of the <i>Yerushalmi</i>. In the main, this is because the influence and prestige of the Jewish community of Israel steadily declined in contrast with the Babylonian community in the years after the redaction of the Talmud and continuing until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaonic" title="Gaonic">Gaonic</a> era. Furthermore, the editing of the Babylonian Talmud was superior to that of the Jerusalem version, making it more accessible and readily usable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AZexile_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-AZexile-25">[22]</a></sup> According to Maimonides (whose life began almost a hundred years after the end of the Gaonic era), all Jewish communities during the Gaonic era formally accepted the Babylonian Talmud as binding upon themselves, and modern Jewish practice follows the Babylonian Talmud's conclusions on all areas in which the two Talmuds conflict.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The structure of the Talmud follows that of the Mishnah, in which six orders (<i>sedarim</i>; singular: <i>seder</i>) of general subject matter are divided into 60 or 63 tractates (<i>masekhtot</i>; singular: <i><a href="/wiki/Masekhet" title="Masekhet">masekhet</a></i>) of more focused subject compilations, though not all tractates have Gemara. Each tractate is divided into chapters (<i>perakim</i>; singular: <i>perek</i>), 517 in total, that are both numbered according to the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</a> and given names, usually using the first one or two words in the first mishnah. A <i>perek</i> may continue over several (up to tens of) <a href="#Printing">pages</a>. Each <i>perek</i> will contain several <i>mishnayot</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mishnah">Mishnah</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mishnah">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> is a compilation of legal opinions and debates. Statements in the Mishnah are typically terse, recording brief opinions of the rabbis debating a subject; or recording only an unattributed ruling, apparently representing a consensus view. The rabbis recorded in the Mishnah are known as the <a href="/wiki/Tannaim" title="Tannaim">Tannaim</a> (literally, "repeaters," or "teachers"). These tannaimârabbis of the second century CE--"who produced the Mishnah and other tannaic works, must be distinguished from the rabbis of the third to fifth centuries, known as amoraim (literally, "speakers"), who produced the two Talmudim and other amoraic works".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since it sequences its laws by subject matter instead of by biblical context, the Mishnah discusses individual subjects more thoroughly than the <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a>, and it includes a much broader selection of halakhic subjects than the Midrash. The Mishnah's topical organization thus became the framework of the Talmud as a whole. But not every tractate in the Mishnah has a corresponding Gemara. Also, the order of the tractates in the Talmud differs in some cases from that in the <i>Mishnah</i>.
</p>
