<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baraita">Baraita</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Baraita">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baraita" title="Baraita">Baraita</a></div>
<p>In addition to the Mishnah, other tannaitic teachings were current at about the same time or shortly after that. The Gemara frequently refers to these tannaitic statements in order to compare them to those contained in the Mishnah and to support or refute the propositions of the <a href="/wiki/Amoraim" title="Amoraim">Amoraim</a>.
</p><p>The <i>baraitot</i> cited in the Gemara are often quotations from the <a href="/wiki/Tosefta" title="Tosefta">Tosefta</a> (a tannaitic compendium of halakha parallel to the Mishnah) and the <a href="/wiki/Midrash_halakha" title="Midrash halakha">Midrash halakha</a> (specifically <a href="/wiki/Midrash#Tannaitic" title="Midrash">Mekhilta, Sifra and Sifre</a>). Some <a href="/wiki/Baraita" title="Baraita"><i>baraitot</i></a>, however, are known only through traditions cited in the Gemara, and are not part of any other collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gemara">Gemara</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Gemara">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gemara" title="Gemara">Gemara</a></div>
<p>In the three centuries following the <a href="/wiki/Redaction" title="Redaction">redaction</a> of the Mishnah, rabbis in Palestine and Babylonia analyzed, debated, and discussed that work. These discussions form the Gemara. The Gemara mainly focuses on elucidating and elaborating the opinions of the Tannaim. The rabbis of the Gemara are known as <span title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic (ca. 200-1200 CE)-language text"><i lang="tmr-Latn">Amoraim</i></span> (sing. <i><span title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic (ca. 200-1200 CE)-language text"><i lang="tmr-Latn">Amora</i></span></i> <span title="Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="he">××××¨×</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of the Gemara consists of legal analysis. The starting point for the analysis is usually a legal statement found in a Mishnah. The statement is then analyzed and compared with other statements used in different <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">approaches</a> to biblical <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a> in rabbinic Judaism (or â simpler â <a href="/wiki/Zohar#pardes" title="Zohar">interpretation</a> of text in <a href="/wiki/Torah_study#Forms_of_traditional_Jewish_Torah_study" title="Torah study">Torah study</a>) exchanges between two (frequently anonymous and sometimes metaphorical) disputants, termed the <i><span title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic (ca. 200-1200 CE)-language text"><i lang="tmr-Latn">makshan</i></span></i> (questioner) and <i><span title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic (ca. 200-1200 CE)-language text"><i lang="tmr-Latn">tartzan</i></span></i> (answerer). Another important function of Gemara is to identify the correct biblical basis for a given law presented in the Mishnah and the logical process connecting one with the other: this activity was known as <i>talmud</i> long before the existence of the "Talmud" as a text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_tractates">Minor tractates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Minor tractates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Minor_tractate" title="Minor tractate">Minor tractate</a></div>
<p>In addition to the six Orders, the Talmud contains a series of short treatises of a later date, usually printed at the end of Seder Nezikin. These are not divided into Mishnah and Gemara.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Within the <a href="/wiki/Gemara" title="Gemara">Gemara</a>, the quotations from the Mishnah and the <a href="/wiki/Baraita" title="Baraita">Baraitas</a> and verses of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> quoted and embedded in the Gemara are in either Mishnaic or Biblical Hebrew. The rest of the Gemara, including the discussions of the Amoraim and the overall framework, is in a characteristic dialect of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Babylonian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic">Jewish Babylonian Aramaic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> There are occasional quotations from older works in other dialects of Aramaic, such as <a href="/wiki/Megillat_Taanit" title="Megillat Taanit">Megillat Taanit</a>. Overall, Hebrew constitutes somewhat less than half of the text of the Talmud.
</p><p>This difference in language is due to the long time period elapsing between the two compilations. During the period of the <a href="/wiki/Tannaim" title="Tannaim">Tannaim</a> (rabbis cited in the Mishnah), a late form of Hebrew known as <a href="/wiki/Mishnaic_Hebrew" title="Mishnaic Hebrew">Rabbinic or Mishnaic Hebrew</a> was still in use as a spoken <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> among Jews in <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judaea</a> (alongside Greek and Aramaic), whereas during the period of the <a href="/wiki/Amoraim" title="Amoraim">Amoraim</a> (rabbis cited in the Gemara), which began around the year 200, the spoken vernacular was almost exclusively Aramaic. Hebrew continued to be used for the writing of religious texts, poetry, and so forth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even within the Aramaic of the Gemara, different dialects or writing styles can be observed in different tractates. One dialect is common to most of the Babylonian Talmud, while a second dialect is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nedarim" title="Nedarim">Nedarim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazir_(Talmud)" title="Nazir (Talmud)">Nazir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temurah_(Talmud)" title="Temurah (Talmud)">Temurah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keritot" title="Keritot">Keritot</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Me%27ilah" title="Me'ilah">Me'ilah</a>; the second dialect is closer in style to the <a href="/wiki/Targum" title="Targum">Targum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Torah_of_Israel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="457" data-file-width="623" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Torah_of_Israel.jpg/220px-Torah_of_Israel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Torah_of_Israel.jpg/330px-Torah_of_Israel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Torah_of_Israel.jpg/440px-Torah_of_Israel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Torah_of_Israel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Talmudic saying on the Divine Presence</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarship">Scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From the time of its completion, the Talmud became integral to Jewish scholarship. A maxim in <a href="/wiki/Pirkei_Avot" title="Pirkei Avot">Pirkei Avot</a> advocates its study from the age of 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> This section outlines some of the major areas of Talmudic study.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geonim">Geonim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geonim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest Talmud commentaries were written by the <a href="/wiki/Geonim" title="Geonim">Geonim</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 800â1000) in <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>. Although some direct commentaries on particular treatises are extant, our main knowledge of the Gaonic era Talmud scholarship comes from statements embedded in Geonic responsa that shed light on Talmudic passages: these are arranged in the order of the Talmud in Levin's <i>Otzar ha-Geonim</i>. Also important are practical abridgments of Jewish law such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yehudai_Gaon" title="Yehudai Gaon">Yehudai Gaon</a>'s <i>Halachot Pesukot</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achai_Gaon" title="Achai Gaon">Achai Gaon</a>'s <i>Sheeltot</i> and <a href="/wiki/Simeon_Kayyara" title="Simeon Kayyara">Simeon Kayyara</a>'s <i>Halachot Gedolot</i>. After the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hai_Gaon" title="Hai Gaon">Hai Gaon</a>, however, the center of Talmud scholarship shifts to Europe and North Africa.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halakhic_and_Aggadic_extractions">Halakhic and Aggadic extractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Halakhic and Aggadic extractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One area of Talmudic scholarship developed out of the need to ascertain the <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakha</a>. Early commentators such as rabbi <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Alfasi" title="Isaac Alfasi">Isaac Alfasi</a> (North Africa, 1013â1103) attempted to extract and determine the binding legal opinions from the vast corpus of the Talmud. Alfasi's work was highly influential, attracted several commentaries in its own right and later served as a basis for the creation of halakhic codes. Another influential medieval Halakhic work following the order of the Babylonian Talmud, and to some extent modelled on Alfasi, was "the <i>Mordechai</i>", a compilation by <a href="/wiki/Mordechai_ben_Hillel" title="Mordechai ben Hillel">Mordechai ben Hillel</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1250â1298). A third such work was that of rabbi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asher_ben_Yechiel" title="Asher ben Yechiel">Asher ben Yechiel</a> (d. 1327). All these works and their commentaries are printed in the Vilna and many subsequent editions of the Talmud.
</p><p>A 15th-century Spanish rabbi, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_ibn_Habib" title="Jacob ibn Habib">Jacob ibn Habib</a> (d. 1516), composed the <i><a href="/wiki/Ein_Yaakov" title="Ein Yaakov">Ein Yaakov</a></i>. <i>Ein Yaakov</i> (or <i>En Ya'aqob</i>) extracts nearly all the <a href="/wiki/Aggadah" title="Aggadah">Aggadic</a> material from the Talmud. It was intended to familiarize the public with the ethical parts of the Talmud and to dispute many of the accusations surrounding its contents.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commentaries">Commentaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Commentaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">Rabbinic literature</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Yeshiva#Talmud_study" title="Yeshiva">Yeshiva Â§Â Talmud study</a></div>
<p>The commentaries on the Talmud constitute only a small part of <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">Rabbinic literature</a> in comparison with the <a href="/wiki/Responsa" title="Responsa">responsa</a> literature and the commentaries on the <a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">codices</a>. When the Talmud was concluded the traditional literature was still so fresh in the memory of scholars that no need existed for writing Talmudic commentaries, nor were such works undertaken in the first period of the <a href="/wiki/Geonim" title="Geonim">gaonate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Paltoi_ben_Abaye" title="Paltoi ben Abaye">Paltoi ben Abaye</a> (<i>c.</i> 840) was the first who in his responsum offered verbal and textual comments on the Talmud. His son, <a href="/wiki/Zemah_ben_Paltoi" title="Zemah ben Paltoi">Zemah ben Paltoi</a> paraphrased and explained the passages which he quoted; and he composed, as an aid to the study of the Talmud, a lexicon which <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zacuto" title="Abraham Zacuto">Abraham Zacuto</a> consulted in the fifteenth century. <a href="/wiki/Saadia_Gaon" title="Saadia Gaon">Saadia Gaon</a> is said to have composed commentaries on the Talmud, aside from his Arabic commentaries on the Mishnah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many passages in the Talmud which are cryptic and difficult to understand. Its language contains many Greek and Persian words that became obscure over time. A major area of Talmudic scholarship developed to explain these passages and words. Some early commentators such as Rabbenu <a href="/wiki/Gershom_ben_Judah" title="Gershom ben Judah">Gershom of Mainz</a> (10th century) and <a href="/wiki/Chananel_ben_Chushiel" title="Chananel ben Chushiel">Rabbenu á¸¤ananel</a> (early 11th century) produced running commentaries to various tractates. These commentaries could be read with the text of the Talmud and would help explain the meaning of the text. Another important work is the <i>Sefer ha-Mafteaá¸¥</i> (Book of the Key) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nissim_Ben_Jacob" title="Nissim Ben Jacob">Nissim Gaon</a>, which contains a preface explaining the different forms of Talmudic argumentation and then explains abbreviated passages in the Talmud by cross-referring to parallel passages where the same thought is expressed in full. Commentaries (<i>á¸¥iddushim</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_ibn_Migash" title="Joseph ibn Migash">Joseph ibn Migash</a> on two tractates, Bava Batra and Shevuot, based on á¸¤ananel and Alfasi, also survive, as does a compilation by <a href="/wiki/Zechariah_Aghmati" title="Zechariah Aghmati">Zechariah Aghmati</a> called <i>Sefer ha-Ner</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> Using a different style, rabbi <a href="/wiki/Nathan_ben_Jehiel" title="Nathan ben Jehiel">Nathan b. Jechiel</a> created a lexicon called the <i>Arukh</i> in the 11th century to help translate difficult words.
</p><p>By far the best-known commentary on the Babylonian Talmud is that of <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a> (Rabbi Solomon ben Isaac, 1040â1105). The commentary is comprehensive, covering almost the entire Talmud. Written as a running commentary, it provides a full explanation of the words and explains the logical structure of each Talmudic passage. It is considered indispensable to students of the Talmud. Although Rashi drew upon all his predecessors, his originality in using the material offered by them was unparalleled. His commentaries, in turn, became the basis of the work of his pupils and successors, who composed a large number of supplementary works that were partly in emendation and partly in explanation of Rashi's, and are known under the title "<a href="/wiki/Tosafot" title="Tosafot">Tosafot</a>." ("additions" or "supplements").
</p><p>The <i>Tosafot</i> are collected commentaries by various medieval Ashkenazic rabbis on the Talmud (known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tosafists" title="Tosafists">Tosafists</a></i> or <i>Ba'alei Tosafot</i>). One of the main goals of the <i>Tosafot</i> is to explain and interpret contradictory statements in the Talmud. Unlike Rashi, the <i>Tosafot</i> is not a running commentary, but rather comments on selected matters. Often the explanations of <i>Tosafot</i>  differ from those of Rashi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Yeshiva, the integration of Talmud, Rashi and Tosafot, is considered as the foundation (and prerequisite) for further analysis; this combination is sometimes referred to by the acronym <i>"gefet"</i> ( ××¤×´×ª - <i><a href="/wiki/Gemara" title="Gemara">Gemara</a></i>,  <i>perush Rashi</i>,  <i>Tosafot</i>).
</p><p>Among the founders of the Tosafist school were Rabbi Jacob ben Meir (known as <a href="/wiki/Rabbeinu_Tam" title="Rabbeinu Tam">Rabbeinu Tam</a>), who was a grandson of Rashi, and, Rabbenu Tam's nephew, rabbi <a href="/wiki/Isaac_ben_Samuel" title="Isaac ben Samuel">Isaac ben Samuel</a>. The Tosafot commentaries were collected in different editions in the various schools. The benchmark collection of Tosafot for Northern France was that of R. <a href="/wiki/Eliezer_of_Touques" title="Eliezer of Touques">Eliezer of Touques</a>. The standard collection for Spain was that of <a href="/wiki/Asher_ben_Jehiel" title="Asher ben Jehiel">Rabbenu Asher</a> ("Tosefot Harosh"). The Tosafot that are printed in the standard Vilna edition of the Talmud are an edited version compiled from the various medieval collections, predominantly that of Touques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over time, the approach of the Tosafists spread to other Jewish communities, particularly those in Spain. This led to the composition of many other commentaries in similar styles. 
Among these are the commentaries of <a href="/wiki/Nachmanides" title="Nachmanides">Nachmanides</a> (Ramban), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solomon_ben_Adret" title="Solomon ben Adret">Solomon ben Adret</a> (Rashba), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yom_Tov_Asevilli" title="Yom Tov Asevilli">Yom Tov of Seville</a> (Ritva) and <a href="/wiki/Nissim_of_Gerona" title="Nissim of Gerona">Nissim of Gerona</a> (Ran); these are often titled â<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiddush" title="Chiddush"><i>Chiddushei</i></a> ...â (â<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/novellae#Noun" title="wikt:novellae">Novellae</a> of ...â).
A comprehensive anthology consisting of extracts from all these is the <i>Shittah Mekubbetzet</i> of <a href="/wiki/Bezalel_Ashkenazi" title="Bezalel Ashkenazi">Bezalel Ashkenazi</a>.
</p><p>Other commentaries produced in Spain and Provence were not influenced by the Tosafist style. Two of the most significant of these are the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yad_Ramah&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yad Ramah (page does not exist)">Yad Ramah</a> by rabbi <a href="/wiki/Meir_Abulafia" title="Meir Abulafia">Meir Abulafia</a> and <i>Bet Habechirah</i> by rabbi <a href="/wiki/Menachem_Meiri" title="Menachem Meiri">Menahem haMeiri</a>, commonly referred to as "Meiri". While the <i>Bet Habechirah</i> is extant for all of Talmud, we only have the <i>Yad Ramah</i> for Tractates Sanhedrin, Baba Batra and Gittin. Like the commentaries of Ramban and the others, these are generally printed as independent works, though some Talmud editions include the <i>Shittah Mekubbetzet</i> in an abbreviated form.
</p><p>In later centuries, focus partially shifted from direct Talmudic interpretation to the analysis of previously written Talmudic commentaries. These later commentaries are generally printed at the back of each tractate.  Well known are "Maharshal" (<a href="/wiki/Solomon_Luria" title="Solomon Luria">Solomon Luria</a>), "Maharam" (<a href="/wiki/Meir_Lublin" title="Meir Lublin">Meir Lublin</a>) and "<a href="/wiki/Maharsha" title="Maharsha">Maharsha</a>" (Samuel Edels), which analyze Rashi and Tosafot together; other such commentaries include <a href="/wiki/Yom-Tov_Lipmann_Heller#Works_and_opinions" title="Yom-Tov Lipmann Heller"><i>Ma'adanei Yom Tov</i></a> by Yom-Tov Lipmann Heller, in turn a commentary on the Rosh (see below), and the glosses by <a href="/wiki/Zvi_Hirsch_Chajes" title="Zvi Hirsch Chajes">Zvi Hirsch Chajes</a>.
</p><p>Another very useful study aid, found in almost all editions of the Talmud, consists of the marginal notes <i>Torah Or</i>, <i>Ein Mishpat Ner Mitzvah</i> and <i>Masoret ha-Shas</i> by the Italian rabbi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joshua_Boaz" title="Joshua Boaz">Joshua Boaz</a>, which give references respectively to the cited Biblical passages, to the relevant halachic codes (<i><a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">Mishneh Torah</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Arba%27ah_Turim" title="Arba'ah Turim">Tur</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shulchan_Aruch" title="Shulchan Aruch">Shulchan Aruch</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Sefer_Mitzvot_Gadol" title="Sefer Mitzvot Gadol">Se'mag</a></i>) and to related Talmudic passages.
</p><p>Most editions of the Talmud include brief marginal notes by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akiva_Eger" title="Akiva Eger">Akiva Eger</a> under the name <i>Gilyon ha-Shas</i>, and textual notes by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joel_Sirkes" title="Joel Sirkes">Joel Sirkes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vilna_Gaon" title="Vilna Gaon">Vilna Gaon</a> (see <a href="#Textual_emendations">Textual emendations</a> below), on the page together with the text.
</p><p>Commentaries discussing the Halachik-legal content include <a href="/wiki/Asher_ben_Jehiel#Works" title="Asher ben Jehiel">"Rosh"</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Alfasi#Works" title="Isaac Alfasi">"Rif"</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mordechai_ben_Hillel#Magnum_opus" title="Mordechai ben Hillel">"Mordechai"</a>; these are now standard appendices to each volume. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rambam" title="Rambam">Rambam</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">Mishneh Torah</a></i> is invariably studied alongside these three; although a code, and therefore not in the same order as the Talmud, the relevant location is identified via the <i>"Ein Mishpat"</i>, as mentioned.
</p><p>A recent project, <i>Halacha Brura</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> founded by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Isaac_Kook" title="Abraham Isaac Kook">Abraham Isaac Kook</a>, presents the Talmud and a summary of the halachic codes side by side, so as to enable the "collation" of Talmud with resultant Halacha.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilpul">Pilpul</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pilpul">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 15th and 16th centuries, a new intensive form of Talmud study arose. Complicated logical arguments were used to explain minor points of contradiction within the Talmud. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Pilpul" title="Pilpul">pilpul</a></i> was applied to this type of study. Usage of <i>pilpul</i> in this sense (that of "sharp analysis") harks back to the Talmudic era and refers to the intellectual sharpness this method demanded.
</p><p>Pilpul practitioners posited that the Talmud could contain no redundancy or contradiction whatsoever. New categories and distinctions (<i>hillukim</i>) were therefore created, resolving seeming contradictions within the Talmud by novel logical means.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi</a> world the founders of <i>pilpul</i> are generally considered to be <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Pollak" title="Jacob Pollak">Jacob Pollak</a> (1460â1541) and <a href="/wiki/Shalom_Shachna" title="Shalom Shachna">Shalom Shachna</a>. This kind of study reached its height in the 16th and 17th centuries when expertise in pilpulistic analysis was considered an art form and became a goal in and of itself within the yeshivot of Poland and Lithuania. But the popular new method of Talmud study was not without critics; already in the 15th century, the ethical tract <i>Orhot Zaddikim</i> ("Paths of the Righteous" in Hebrew) criticized pilpul for an overemphasis on intellectual acuity. Many 16th- and 17th-century rabbis were also critical of pilpul. Among them are <a href="/wiki/Judah_Loew_ben_Bezalel" title="Judah Loew ben Bezalel">Judah Loew ben Bezalel</a> (the <i>Maharal</i> of Prague), <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Horowitz" title="Isaiah Horowitz">Isaiah Horowitz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yair_Bacharach" title="Yair Bacharach">Yair Bacharach</a>.
</p><p>By the 18th century, pilpul study waned. Other styles of learning such as that of the school of Elijah b. Solomon, the <a href="/wiki/Vilna_Gaon" title="Vilna Gaon">Vilna Gaon</a>, became popular. The term "pilpul" was increasingly applied derogatorily to novellae deemed casuistic and hairsplitting. Authors referred to their own commentaries as "al derekh ha-peshat" (by the simple method)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> to contrast them with pilpul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sephardic_approaches">Sephardic approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sephardic approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Among <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">Sephardi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_Jews" title="Italian Jews">Italian Jews</a> from the 15th century on, some authorities sought to apply the methods of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_logic" title="Aristotelian logic">Aristotelian logic</a>, as reformulated by <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> This method was first recorded, though without explicit reference to Aristotle, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isaac_Campanton" title="Isaac Campanton">Isaac Campanton</a> (d. Spain, 1463) in his <i>Darkhei ha-Talmud</i> ("The Ways of the Talmud"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> and is also found in the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moses_Chaim_Luzzatto" title="Moses Chaim Luzzatto">Moses Chaim Luzzatto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the present-day Sephardi scholar <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Faur" title="JosÃ© Faur">JosÃ© Faur</a>, traditional Sephardic Talmud study could take place on any of three levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The most basic level consists of literary analysis of the text without the help of commentaries, designed to bring out the <i>tzurata di-shema'ta</i>, i.e. the logical and narrative structure of the passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>The intermediate level, <i>iyyun</i> (concentration), consists of study with the help of commentaries such as <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tosafot" title="Tosafot">Tosafot</a>, similar to that practiced among the <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> Historically Sephardim studied the <i>Tosefot ha-Rosh</i> and the commentaries of Nahmanides in preference to the printed Tosafot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> A method based on the study of Tosafot, and of Ashkenazi authorities such as <i><a href="/wiki/Maharsha" title="Maharsha">Maharsha</a></i> (Samuel Edels) and <i>Maharshal</i> (<a href="/wiki/Solomon_Luria" title="Solomon Luria">Solomon Luria</a>), was introduced in late seventeenth century <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Tunisia" title="History of the Jews in Tunisia">Tunisia</a> by rabbis Abraham Hakohen (d. 1715) and Tsemaá¸¥ Tsarfati (d. 1717) and perpetuated by rabbi <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Lumbroso" title="Isaac Lumbroso">Isaac Lumbroso</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> and is sometimes referred to as<i> 'Iyyun Tunisa'i</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li>The highest level, <i>halachah</i> (Jewish law), consists of collating the opinions set out in the Talmud with those of the halachic codes such as the <a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">Mishneh Torah</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shulchan_Aruch" title="Shulchan Aruch">Shulchan Aruch</a>, so as to study the Talmud as a source of law; the equivalent Ashkenazi approach is sometimes referred to as being "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aliba_dehilchasa" title="Aliba dehilchasa">aliba dehilchasa</a>".</li></ul>
<p>Today most Sephardic yeshivot follow Lithuanian approaches such as the Brisker method: the traditional Sephardic methods are perpetuated informally by some individuals.<i> 'Iyyun Tunisa'i</i> is taught at the <a href="/wiki/Kisse_Rahamim_yeshivah" title="Kisse Rahamim yeshivah">Kisse Rahamim yeshivah</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bnei_Brak" title="Bnei Brak">Bnei Brak</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brisker_method">Brisker method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Brisker method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the late 19th century another trend in Talmud study arose. Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Chaim_Soloveitchik" title="Chaim Soloveitchik">Hayyim Soloveitchik</a> (1853â1918) of Brisk (Brest-Litovsk) developed and refined this style of study. <a href="/wiki/Brisker_method" title="Brisker method">Brisker method</a> involves a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reductionist" title="Reductionist">reductionistic</a> analysis of rabbinic arguments within the Talmud or among the <a href="/wiki/Rishonim" title="Rishonim">Rishonim</a>, explaining the differing opinions by placing them within a categorical structure. The Brisker method is highly analytical and is often criticized as being a modern-day version of <a href="/wiki/Pilpul" title="Pilpul">pilpul</a>. Nevertheless, the influence of the Brisker method is great. Most modern-day Yeshivot study the Talmud using the Brisker method in some form. One feature of this method is the use of <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">Mishneh Torah</a></i> as a guide to Talmudic interpretation, as distinct from its use as a source of practical <i>halakha</i>.
</p><p>Rival methods were those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mir_yeshiva_(Poland)" title="Mir yeshiva (Poland)">Mir</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telz_yeshiva" title="Telz yeshiva">Telz yeshivas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup>
See <a href="/wiki/Chaim_Rabinowitz#Telshe" title="Chaim Rabinowitz">Chaim Rabinowitz Â§Â Telshe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yeshiva_Ohel_Torah-Baranovich#Style_of_learning" title="Yeshiva Ohel Torah-Baranovich">Yeshiva Ohel Torah-Baranovich Â§Â Style of learning</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_method">Critical method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Critical method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a result of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_emancipation" title="Jewish emancipation">Jewish emancipation</a>, Judaism underwent enormous upheaval and transformation during the 19th century. Modern methods of textual and historical analysis were applied to the Talmud.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_emendations">Textual emendations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Textual emendations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The text of the Talmud has been subject to some level of critical scrutiny throughout its history. Rabbinic tradition holds that the people cited in both Talmuds did not have a hand in its writings; rather, their teachings were edited into a rough form around 450 CE (Talmud Yerushalmi) and 550 CE (Talmud Bavli.) The text of the Bavli especially was not firmly fixed at that time.
</p><p>Gaonic responsa literature addresses this issue. Teshuvot Geonim Kadmonim, section 78, deals with mistaken biblical readings in the Talmud.  This Gaonic responsum states:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... But you must examine carefully in every case when you feel uncertainty [as to the credibility of the text] â what is its source? Whether a scribal error? Or the superficiality of a second rate student who was not well versed?....after the manner of many mistakes found among those superficial second-rate students, and certainly among those rural memorizers who were not familiar with the biblical text. And since they erred in the first place... [they compounded the error.]</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite>Teshuvot Geonim Kadmonim, Ed. Cassel, Berlin 1858, Photographic reprint Tel Aviv 1964, 23b.</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In the early medieval era, Rashi already concluded that some statements in the extant text of the Talmud were insertions from later editors. On Shevuot 3b Rashi writes "A mistaken student wrote this in the margin of the Talmud, and copyists [subsequently] put it into the Gemara."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[d]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emendations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yoel_Sirkis" title="Yoel Sirkis">Yoel Sirkis</a> and the Vilna Gaon are included in all standard editions of the Talmud, in the form of marginal glosses entitled <i>Hagahot ha-Bach</i> and <i>Hagahot ha-Gra</i> respectively; further emendations by <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Luria" title="Solomon Luria">Solomon Luria</a> are set out in commentary form at the back of each tractate. The Vilna Gaon's emendations were often based on his quest for internal consistency in the text rather than on manuscript evidence;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup> nevertheless many of the Gaon's emendations were later verified by textual critics, such as <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Schechter" title="Solomon Schechter">Solomon Schechter</a>, who had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cairo_Genizah" title="Cairo Genizah">Cairo Genizah</a> texts with which to compare our standard editions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Nathan_Nota_Rabinovicz" title="Raphael Nathan Nota Rabinovicz">Raphael Nathan Nota Rabinovicz</a> published a multi-volume work entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Dikdukei_Soferim" title="Dikdukei Soferim">Dikdukei Soferim</a></i>, showing textual variants from the Munich and other early manuscripts of the Talmud, and further variants are recorded in the Complete Israeli Talmud and <i>Gemara Shelemah</i> editions (see <a href="#Critical_editions">Critical editions</a>, above).
</p><p>Today many more manuscripts have become available, in particular from the <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Geniza" title="Cairo Geniza">Cairo Geniza</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_the_Hebrew_Language" title="Academy of the Hebrew Language">Academy of the Hebrew Language</a> has prepared a text on CD-ROM for lexicographical purposes, containing the text of each tractate according to the manuscript it considers most reliable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup> and images of some of the older manuscripts may be found on the website of the <a href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Israel" title="National Library of Israel">National Library of Israel</a> (formerly the Jewish National and University Library).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> The NLI, the Lieberman Institute (associated with the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Theological_Seminary_of_America" title="Jewish Theological Seminary of America">Jewish Theological Seminary of America</a>), the Institute for the Complete Israeli Talmud (part of Yad Harav Herzog) and the Friedberg Jewish Manuscript Society all maintain searchable websites on which the viewer can request variant manuscript readings of a given passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further variant readings can often be gleaned from citations in secondary literature such as commentaries, in particular, those of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Alfasi" title="Isaac Alfasi">Alfasi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chananel_ben_Chushiel" title="Chananel ben Chushiel">Rabbenu á¸¤ananel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zechariah_Aghmati" title="Zechariah Aghmati">Aghmati</a>, and sometimes the later Spanish commentators such as <a href="/wiki/Nachmanides" title="Nachmanides">Nachmanides</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solomon_ben_Adret" title="Solomon ben Adret">Solomon ben Adret</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Historical_analysis.2C_and_higher_textual_criticism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_analysis,_and_higher_textual_criticism">Historical analysis, and higher textual criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Historical analysis, and higher textual criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Historical study of the Talmud can be used to investigate a variety of concerns. One can ask questions such as: Do a given section's sources date from its editor's lifetime? To what extent does a section have earlier or later sources? Are Talmudic disputes distinguishable along theological or communal lines? In what ways do different sections derive from different schools of thought within early Judaism? Can these early sources be identified, and if so, how?  Investigation of questions such as these are known as <i>higher textual criticism</i>. (The term "criticism" is a technical term denoting academic study.)
</p><p>Religious scholars still debate the precise method by which the text of the Talmuds reached their final form. Many believe that the text was continuously smoothed over by the <i><a href="/wiki/Savoraim" title="Savoraim">savoraim</a></i>.
</p><p>In the 1870s and 1880s, rabbi Raphael Natan Nata Rabbinovitz engaged in the historical study of Talmud Bavli in his <i>Diqduqei Soferim</i>. Since then many Orthodox rabbis have approved of his work, including Rabbis <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Kluger" title="Shlomo Kluger">Shlomo Kluger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Saul_Nathansohn" title="Joseph Saul Nathansohn">Joseph Saul Nathansohn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Ettlinger" title="Jacob Ettlinger">Jacob Ettlinger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yitzchak_Elchanan_Spektor" title="Yitzchak Elchanan Spektor">Isaac Elhanan Spektor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shimon_Sofer" title="Shimon Sofer">Shimon Sofer</a>.
</p><p>During the early 19th century, leaders of the newly evolving <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform movement</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Geiger" title="Abraham Geiger">Abraham Geiger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Holdheim" title="Samuel Holdheim">Samuel Holdheim</a>, subjected the Talmud to severe scrutiny as part of an effort to break with traditional rabbinic Judaism. They insisted that the Talmud was entirely a work of evolution and development. This view was rejected as both academically incorrect, and religiously incorrect, by those who would become known as the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox movement</a>. Some Orthodox leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Moses_Sofer" title="Moses Sofer">Moses Sofer</a> (the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>) became exquisitely sensitive to any change and rejected modern critical methods of Talmud study.
</p><p>Some rabbis advocated a view of Talmudic study that they held to be in-between the Reformers and the Orthodox; these were the adherents of positive-historical Judaism, notably <a href="/wiki/Nachman_Krochmal" title="Nachman Krochmal">Nachman Krochmal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zecharias_Frankel" title="Zecharias Frankel">Zecharias Frankel</a>. They described the <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">Oral Torah</a> as the result of a historical and exegetical process, emerging over time, through the application of authorized exegetical techniques, and more importantly, the subjective dispositions and personalities and current historical conditions, by learned sages. This was later developed more fully in the five-volume work <i>Dor Dor ve-Dorshav</i> by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Hirsch_Weiss" title="Isaac Hirsch Weiss">Isaac Hirsch Weiss</a>. (See Jay Harris <i>Guiding the Perplexed in the Modern Age</i> Ch. 5) Eventually, their work came to be one of the formative parts of <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">Conservative Judaism</a>.
</p><p>Another aspect of this movement is reflected in <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Graetz" title="Heinrich Graetz">Graetz</a>'s <i>History of the Jews</i>. Graetz attempts to deduce the personality of the <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisees</a> based on the laws or aggadot that they cite, and show that their personalities influenced the laws they expounded.
</p><p>The leader of Orthodox Jewry in Germany <a href="/wiki/Samson_Raphael_Hirsch" title="Samson Raphael Hirsch">Samson Raphael Hirsch</a>, while not rejecting the methods of scholarship in principle, hotly contested the findings of the Historical-Critical method. In a series of articles in his magazine <i>Jeschurun</i> (reprinted in Collected Writings Vol. 5) Hirsch reiterated the traditional view and pointed out what he saw as numerous errors in the works of Graetz, Frankel and Geiger.
</p><p>On the other hand, many of the 19th century's strongest critics of Reform, including strictly orthodox rabbis such as <a href="/wiki/Zvi_Hirsch_Chajes" title="Zvi Hirsch Chajes">Zvi Hirsch Chajes</a>, utilized this new scientific method. The Orthodox rabbinical seminary of <a href="/wiki/Azriel_Hildesheimer" title="Azriel Hildesheimer">Azriel Hildesheimer</a> was founded on the idea of creating a "harmony between Judaism and science". Other Orthodox pioneers of scientific Talmud study were <a href="/wiki/David_Zvi_Hoffmann" title="David Zvi Hoffmann">David Zvi Hoffmann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hirsch_D%C3%BCnner" title="Joseph Hirsch DÃ¼nner">Joseph Hirsch DÃ¼nner</a>.
</p><p>The Iraqi rabbi <a href="/wiki/Yaakov_Chaim_Sofer" title="Yaakov Chaim Sofer">Yaakov Chaim Sofer</a> notes that the text of the Gemara has had changes and additions, and contains statements not of the same origin as the original. See his <i>Yehi Yosef</i> (Jerusalem, 1991) p.Â 132 "This passage does not bear the signature of the editor of the Talmud!"
</p><p>Orthodox scholar <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Sperber" title="Daniel Sperber">Daniel Sperber</a> writes in "Legitimacy, of Necessity, of Scientific Disciplines" that many Orthodox sources have engaged in the historical (also called "scientific") study of the Talmud. As such, the divide today between Orthodoxy and Reform is not about whether the Talmud may be subjected to historical study, but rather about the theological and halakhic implications of such study.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_scholarship">Contemporary scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Contemporary scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some trends within contemporary Talmud scholarship are listed below.
</p>
<ul><li>Orthodox Judaism maintains that the <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">oral Torah</a> was revealed, in some form, together with the written Torah. As such, some adherents, most notably <a href="/wiki/Samson_Raphael_Hirsch" title="Samson Raphael Hirsch">Samson Raphael Hirsch</a> and his followers, resisted any effort to apply historical methods that imputed specific motives to the authors of the Talmud. Other major figures in Orthodoxy, however, took issue with Hirsch on this matter, most prominently <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Tzvi_Hoffman" title="David Tzvi Hoffman">David Tzvi Hoffmann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li>Some scholars hold that there has been extensive editorial reshaping of the stories and statements within the Talmud. Lacking outside confirming texts, they hold that we cannot confirm the origin or date of most statements and laws, and that we can say little for certain about their authorship. In this view, the questions above are impossible to answer. See, for example, the works of <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacobs" title="Louis Jacobs">Louis Jacobs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaye_J.D._Cohen" title="Shaye J.D. Cohen">Shaye J.D. Cohen</a>.</li>
<li>Some scholars hold that the Talmud has been extensively shaped by later editorial redaction, but that it contains sources we can identify and describe with some level of reliability. In this view, sources can be identified by tracing the history and analyzing the geographical regions of origin. See, for example, the works of <a href="/wiki/Lee_I._Levine" title="Lee I. Levine">Lee I. Levine</a> and David Kraemer.</li>
<li>Some scholars hold that many or most of the statements and events described in the Talmud usually occurred more or less as described, and that they can be used as serious sources of historical study. In this view, historians do their best to tease out later editorial additions (itself a very difficult task) and skeptically view accounts of miracles, leaving behind a reliable historical text. See, for example, the works of <a href="/wiki/Saul_Lieberman" title="Saul Lieberman">Saul Lieberman</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Weiss_Halivni" title="David Weiss Halivni">David Weiss Halivni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Avraham_Goldberg" title="Avraham Goldberg">Avraham Goldberg</a>.</li>
<li>Modern academic study attempts to separate the different "strata" within the text, to try to interpret each level on its own, and to identify the correlations between parallel versions of the same tradition. In recent years, the works of R. <a href="/wiki/David_Weiss_Halivni" title="David Weiss Halivni">David Weiss Halivni</a> and Dr. Shamma Friedman have suggested a paradigm shift in the understanding of the Talmud (Encyclopaedia Judaica 2nd ed. entry "Talmud, Babylonian"). The traditional understanding was to view the Talmud as a unified homogeneous work. While other scholars had also treated the Talmud as a multi-layered work, Dr. Halivni's innovation (primarily in the second volume of his <i>Mekorot u-Mesorot</i>) was to differentiate between the Amoraic statements, which are generally brief Halachic decisions or inquiries, and the writings of the later "Stammaitic" (or Saboraic) authors, which are characterised by a much longer analysis that often consists of lengthy dialectic discussion. The Jerusalem Talmud is very similar to the Babylonian Talmud minus Stammaitic activity (Encyclopaedia Judaica (2nd ed.), entry "Jerusalem Talmud"). Shamma Y. Friedman's <i>Talmud Aruch</i> on the sixth chapter of Bava Metzia (1996) is the first example of a complete analysis of a Talmudic text using this method. S. Wald has followed with works on Pesachim ch. 3 (2000) and Shabbat ch. 7 (2006). Further commentaries in this sense are being published by Dr Friedman's "Society for the Interpretation of the Talmud".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Some scholars are indeed using outside sources to help give historical and contextual understanding of certain areas of the Babylonian Talmud. See for example the works of the Prof Yaakov Elman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup> and of his student Dr. Shai Secunda,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup> which seek to place the Talmud in its Iranian context, for example by comparing it with contemporary <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian</a> texts.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translations">Translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Talmud_Bavli">Talmud Bavli</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Talmud Bavli">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile nowraplinks"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-pretitle">Part of a series of articles on</td></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-title-with-pretitle" style="padding:0 0.2 em; font-size:120%; font-weight:normal;">Editions of the <a href="/wiki/Talmud#Babylonian_Talmud" title="Talmud">Babylonian Talmud</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Talmud_Set.png" title="Talmud"><img alt="Talmud" data-file-height="462" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="66" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Talmud_Set.png/100px-Talmud_Set.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Talmud_Set.png/150px-Talmud_Set.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Talmud_Set.png/200px-Talmud_Set.png 2x" width="100"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa; background:transparent; text-align:left; font-weight:bold; padding-left:5px; padding-top:3px;"><a href="/wiki/Talmud#Talmud_Bavli" title="Talmud">Editions:</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content hlist">

<li><a href="/wiki/Talmud#Talmud_Bavli" title="Talmud">Rodkinson Translation</a></li>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Editions_of_the_Talmud" title="Template:Editions of the Talmud"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Editions_of_the_Talmud" title="Template talk:Editions of the Talmud"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Editions_of_the_Talmud&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are six contemporary translations of the Talmud into English:
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Steinsaltz">Steinsaltz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Steinsaltz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1683" data-file-width="1304" decoding="async" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg/220px-Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg/330px-Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg/440px-Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Koren_Talmud_Bavli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Koren Talmud Bavli</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The NoÃ© Edition of the <i>Koren Talmud Bavli</i>, <a href="/wiki/Adin_Steinsaltz" title="Adin Steinsaltz">Adin Steinsaltz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koren_Publishers_Jerusalem" title="Koren Publishers Jerusalem">Koren Publishers Jerusalem</a> was launched in 2012. It has a new, modern English translation and the commentary of rabbi <a href="/wiki/Adin_Steinsaltz" title="Adin Steinsaltz">Adin Steinsaltz</a>, and was praised for its "beautiful page" with "clean type".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup> Opened from the right cover (front for Hebrew and Aramaic books), <a href="/wiki/The_Talmud:_The_Steinsaltz_Edition" title="The Talmud: The Steinsaltz Edition">the Steinsaltz Talmud</a> edition has the traditional Vilna page with vowels and punctuation in the original Aramaic text. The <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a> commentary appears in <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">Rashi script</a> with vowels and punctuation. When opened from the left cover the edition features bilingual text with side-by-side English/Aramaic translation. The margins include color maps, illustrations and notes based on rabbi <a href="/wiki/Adin_Steinsaltz" title="Adin Steinsaltz">Adin Steinsaltz</a>âs <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew language</a> translation and commentary of the Talmud. Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Tzvi_Hersh_Weinreb" title="Tzvi Hersh Weinreb">Tzvi Hersh Weinreb</a> serves as the Editor-in-Chief. The entire set, which has vowels and punctuation (including for Rashi) is 42 volumes.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Talmud:_The_Steinsaltz_Edition" title="The Talmud: The Steinsaltz Edition">The Talmud: The Steinsaltz Edition</a></i> (Random House) contains the text with punctuation and an English translation based on Rabbi Steinsaltz' complete <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew language</a> translation of and commentary on the entire Talmud. Incompleteâ22 volumes and a reference guide. There are two formats: one with the traditional Vilna page and one without. It is available in modern Hebrew (first volume published 1969), English (first volume published 1989), French, Russian and other languages.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Artscroll">Artscroll</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Artscroll">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Schottenstein_Edition_of_the_Babylonian_Talmud" title="Schottenstein Edition of the Babylonian Talmud">Schottenstein Edition of the Talmud</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artscroll" title="Artscroll">Artscroll</a>/Mesorah Publications), is 73 volumes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> both with English translation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> and the Aramaic/Hebrew only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup> In the translated editions, each English page faces the Aramaic/Hebrew page it translates. Each Aramaic/Hebrew page of Talmud typically requires three to six English pages of translation and notes. The Aramaic/Hebrew pages are printed in the traditional Vilna format, with a gray bar added that shows the section translated on the facing page. The facing pages provide an expanded paraphrase in English, with translation of the text shown in bold and explanations interspersed in normal type, along with extensive footnotes. Pages are numbered in the traditional way but with a superscript added, e.g. 12b<sup>4</sup> is the fourth page translating the Vilna page 12b. Larger tractates require multiple volumes. The first volume was published in 1990, and the series was completed in 2004.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soncino">Soncino</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Talmud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Soncino">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><i>The Soncino Talmud</i> (1935-1948),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isidore_Epstein" title="Isidore Epstein">Isidore Epstein</a>, Soncino Press (26 volumes; also formerly an 18 volume edition was published).  Notes on each page provide additional background material.
